**Stream B — Community.** This is the stream most observers underweight. Subject maintains active casework — informal, off-the-books, descended from his social-work practice. He runs a phone line (a number circulated by word of mouth in a handful of communities, principally but not exclusively Harlem and the Delta region around Cypress Hills/Mississippi where extended family remains), takes calls in scheduled blocks (typically 1100–1230 and 1700–1830 local), and works problems that range from a teenager in legal trouble to a woman trying to leave a household to a pastor in a different city asking advice on a youth program. Subject takes notes by hand on yellow legal pads; pads are shredded weekly. He does not log these calls in any institutional system. This stream is invisible to most of the agencies that think they understand him and is, in his own internal accounting, the *primary* job. The cape is what funds it and what occasionally lets him answer a call by flying somewhere.

**Stream C — Mentorship.** Standing weekly contact with Joaquin Torres, irregular but reliable contact with a half-dozen younger operators and community organizers. The Torres contact is a structured debrief — Subject asks more questions than he answers, lets Torres talk through his week, intervenes only at decision points. The pattern replicates almost exactly the supervisory style of a senior social-work clinician with a junior caseworker, which is to say: developmental, non-directive, patient with mistakes, firm about ethics. Subject will spend ninety minutes on a call with Torres about a decision Torres has already made, because the *processing* is the point.

**Midday.** Subject eats lunch at a table, not at a desk, even when the table is in a hangar break room. This is non-negotiable and has been remarked on by multiple co-workers across decades. Food is unfussy, often a sandwich he made that morning and carried in. Coffee in the afternoon is replaced by water; he does not run on caffeine past 1300.

**Afternoon.** Operational stream typically dominates afternoons when the tempo is up; community stream dominates when it is down. The flexibility is real but the *containers* are fixed — Subject is the rare principal whose schedule has slack built into it, and he protects the slack as fiercely as he protects the appointments.

---

### 6.3 Habits of Place

**Residence pattern.** Subject keeps a small primary residence (Harlem-anchored across most of the canonical record, with rotating secondary footholds in DC, Brooklyn, and at various points a family-adjacent place in the Delta) and travels light between them. Residences are kept *clean* in the operational sense: minimal personal artifact density, photographs of family but not of the work, books in evidence, a kitchen that is actually used. He does not own much. What he owns, he maintains.

**The window.** Every residence Subject has occupied for more than a few weeks acquires a "window" — a single chosen vantage, usually east-facing, where he stands in the morning and sometimes in the evening. In Harlem it overlooks a particular intersection; in DC it overlooked a tree line. Surveillance optics would catch him at this window for two to six minutes at a time, several times a day, doing nothing observable. Assessment: this is *seeing the board*. It is the same cognitive practice that the bird's-eye link externalizes in the field — Subject orients to space by holding still in it.

**Movement through the city.** When not in costume, Subject walks. He has a strong preference for walking over driving and over rideshare, and his walking routes through Harlem are *patterned* in the way a beat cop's are — he covers a rotating set of blocks weekly, knows the merchants on those blocks, stops at specific stoops, eats at a small set of places where he is known and where the kitchen knows what he orders. This pattern is intelligence-gathering as second nature; it is also community presence. He is *seen* on these blocks deliberately. A teenager who needs to find him knows which corners to walk past on which afternoons.

**Restaurants and counters.** Subject is a counter-sitter, not a table-sitter, when alone. Sits with his back not quite to the wall — angled so the door is in peripheral view but not so obviously placed that the posture announces itself. Tips generously and in cash. Knows the names of staff. Will eat alone in public without a phone or book in front of him perhaps a third of the time — Subject is unusually comfortable being unaccompanied without armor against being seen.

**Travel.** When traveling on his own dime, commercial, aisle seat, carry-on only, arrives at the gate late enough to skip the holding pen, polite to gate agents in a way that reads as genuine rather than performative. When traveling on institutional dime, accepts the upgrade without ceremony. Hotel routine: kettle, the moka pot from the duffel, repacks the kit immediately on arrival rather than leaving it to the morning of departure, hangs one shirt only.

---

### 6.4 Habits of Time

**The week, structured.** Sunday is the protected day — no operational commitments accepted absent emergency, no scheduled meetings, family calls in the late morning, a longer walk, often a meal cooked for someone (Sarah's family when geography permits, otherwise a neighbor, otherwise Barnes, who will eat anything and say nothing). Sunday evening Subject reads for two to three uninterrupted hours and turns in early. Monday is the heaviest operational day by choice — he front-loads the week. Wednesday is the community-stream-heavy day. Friday evening is reserved for the small circle when possible — Misty when she is in town, Barnes when he can be coaxed, Rogers when the orbits align. Saturday morning training is the longest of the week; Saturday afternoon is errands and unstructured time, the *only* genuinely unstructured block in the schedule, and even it tends to fill with low-stakes neighborhood encounters.

**The year, structured.** Two dates in the calendar produce observable behavioral shift: the anniversary of his father's death and the anniversary of his mother's. On both, Subject travels to specific locations — a church in Harlem in one case, a cemetery in the other — alone, on foot or by train, without security. He does not announce these trips. He does not post-process them in conversation with anyone in the surveillance record. He returns home and cooks. The day after each anniversary he is observably quieter, kinder to strangers in a slightly more deliberate way, and trains for a longer block than usual the following morning.

A third date — variable, but tied to the Snap revelation period — produces a fainter version of the same signature. Subject himself may not consciously track it; the surveillance record does.

---

### 6.5 Downtime, Pleasures, Tells

**Reading.** Subject reads broadly — history (particularly American social and labor history, with a marked concentration on the long Black freedom struggle and the post-Reconstruction South), biography, occasional fiction (favors Black American novelists and the kind of mid-century journalism that reads like novels), policy nonfiction on policing, housing, and youth services. He reads slowly, with a pencil, and dog-ears. He keeps books after he has finished them and is willing to give them away. He does not read superhero memoirs; this is consistent and almost certainly a conscious choice.

**Music.** Plays music in the kitchen and while training, not while reading or working. Range is wide and generationally inflected — gospel inherited from his father's household, soul and funk from his own coming-of-age, jazz that he came to later and takes seriously, hip-hop that he listens to with the attention of a man who wants to understand what young people in his neighborhood are actually saying. He sings under his breath while cooking. He does not dance in public; he does dance, briefly and badly, in the kitchen.

**Hands.** Subject's hands are almost always doing something small — folding a dish towel, turning a coin, working a hand-grip trainer, tightening the strap of a watch. The fidgets are *low-amplitude* and *contained*, not anxious-looking; they are the manual equivalent of his verbal patience, a way of bleeding off energy without showing it. Under genuine stress the fidgets *stop*. Stillness in Subject's hands during conversation is the tell that the conversation has become serious.

**Eyes.** Subject's gaze in conversation is steady, with frequent brief breaks downward — not avoidant, more like he is consulting something — and he tracks rooms with a regular cadence even in safe environments. In a restaurant the cadence is approximately every ninety seconds; in an unfamiliar room, every twenty. He is not aware he is doing this and would be mildly annoyed to be told.

**Voice.** Conversational baseline is warm, mid-register, unhurried; he uses humor early in encounters to set the register and de-escalates without announcing the de-escalation. The shift into operational speech is *acoustically distinct*: pitch drops slightly, cadence regularizes, contractions decrease, sentences shorten. The shift back is gradual and signals the situation is concluded. Subject's laugh is genuine and used economically — he is not a performer of mirth; when he laughs, something was funny.

**Phone.** Carries one phone, plus a second for the community line. Both go on a shelf by the door when he is at home for the evening. He does not scroll. He uses the phone as a tool and puts it down. This is unusual enough in the contemporary baseline that it counts as a discipline.

**Clothing.** Off-duty wardrobe is small, considered, and consistent — well-cut but unfussy, slightly more formal than the room expects, with a preference for earth tones and good shoes. He owns one good suit and keeps it pressed. The wardrobe reads as a man dressed by his own taste rather than by a stylist, and as a man who learned early that a Black professional is read on sight and chose to be read clearly.

**Sleep.** Reliably seven to eight hours when conditions allow. Reads in bed for fifteen to thirty minutes before lights out. Sleeps on his side, the same side, in any bed. Wakes once briefly most nights and goes back down within minutes. The sleep architecture, by every available indirect indicator, is genuinely sound — itself a remarkable finding given the cumulative trauma load.

---

### 6.6 The Hypothetical Surveillance Week, Composited

What a continuous-coverage package would actually contain, in summary form:

A man who rises before the city does, who stands at a window for two minutes and says something under his breath that no microphone has clearly captured, who trains with the seriousness of a professional and the economy of a man with nothing to prove, who cooks his own breakfast and washes the pan, who answers his sister's text before he answers the Director's, who walks the same set of blocks every week and is greeted by name on every one of them, who keeps three jobs braided into one schedule and refuses to confuse them, who eats lunch at a table, who takes calls from strangers on a phone line nobody pays him for, who reads with a pencil, who tips in cash, who laughs when something is funny, who goes still when something is wrong, who is in bed by 2230 with a book, who can be roused from sleep into full operational competence in under sixty seconds and who, having done that and survived, will be back at the same window the following morning at the same time, doing the same two minutes of nothing.

The picture this composites is of a man whose interior order is *practiced* rather than given. The routine is not rigidity; it is the daily reconstruction of a self that the world has repeatedly tried to reassign. Every morning's window, every washed pan, every walked block, every kept appointment, every Sunday refused to the institution — all of it is the same act, performed in different media: *I decide what this day is.* In a file built to understand rather than to control, this is the most important single finding in the section.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: SAMUEL THOMAS WILSON
## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

The family file is, in Sam's case, disproportionately a file of the dead. The living branch is thin — a sister, a nephew or two depending on the era of record, an estranged or semi-estranged older brother — and the dead branch is foundational: two parents lost violently in childhood, an aunt-figure and grandparent layer barely sketched in canon, and a vocational lineage (the Black Harlem ministerial-and-social-service tradition) that functions, for Sam, as a kind of extended kin. What follows maps the people, not the symbols.

---

### 5.1 — Paul Wilson (father, deceased)

**History.** Reverend Paul Wilson of Harlem: ordained Baptist minister, congregation small, vocation public. The canon (consistent across Englehart, Priest, Brubaker, Spencer, Coates) places him as the kind of preacher who treated the pulpit as a half-time job and the street as the other half — a clergyman who walked the block, knew the corner kids by name, and inserted himself bodily into neighborhood disputes. He died trying to break up a fight between two parties in the street outside (or near) his church when Sam was a young boy — variously placed between ages seven and nine. The death was incidental in the police-report sense (he was not the target) and intentional in the moral sense (he chose to step into the violence).

**Dynamics in life.** What Sam remembers, and what he selects to retell, is sermonic: the father as a *voice*, a man who taught by talking, who used words to make people sit down. The granular detail Sam returns to in private moments — invoked across writers in the cadence of his own speeches, his Spencer-era public addresses, his Coates-era voiceover — is the rhythm of his father's preaching, not the content. Sam internalized the *manner* of his father (the controlled tenor, the willingness to name a thing in the room, the use of humor to lower a temperature before raising a point) more than any specific theology. He does not, on the record, attend church as an adult with any regularity. The inheritance is not religious; it is **rhetorical and somatic**.

**Evolution.** The relationship has evolved entirely posthumously, which is to say it has done all its work in Sam's interpretation of it. Three phases:

1. *The hagiographic phase* (childhood through young adulthood): father as martyr, the unambiguous moral model. This is the phase that produced the social-work vocation.
2. *The interrogative phase* (Snap-retcon era forward): if Sam's gentleness was implanted, then the very identification with his father was suspect — was the kind-son self-concept a Cosmic Cube artifact or a genuine inheritance? Sam's working answer, derivable from his choices, is that the inheritance is genuine because he keeps *choosing* it; the test of authentic patrimony is repetition under conditions where it would be easier not to.
3. *The peer phase* (Coates run onward, Sam now older than his father was at death): the father reframed not as titan but as a particular man who made a particular choice in a particular street, with a wife and small children at home — i.e., a man whose courage is reassessed as also carrying a cost Sam is now old enough to weigh. There is a faint, never-articulated current in mature-era Sam of *critique* of his father: the man who stepped into the fight left the family undefended for what came next.

**Trust / safety.** Internalized as absolute and never tested in life. The father is the residual *secure-base* figure in Sam's internal working model; the earned-secure attachment style maps to a child who had a genuinely safe early caregiver and then lost him. The safety is not generalized to clergymen, to authority, or to institutions — it is specific to the remembered man.

**Failure mode.** When Sam over-functions — when he absorbs structural disrespect rather than negotiating it, when he stays in a role past the point of personal cost — the underlying script is *paternal*: the father who stepped into the fight that was not his to step into and did not come home. Sam's most self-destructive operational pattern is a re-enactment of the moment he lost his father, and he is competent enough that he keeps surviving it.

**Leverage.** Anyone who could credibly speak in or about Paul Wilson's voice — produce a recording, a sermon transcript, a remembered phrase from a parishioner — would have Sam's full attention in a way nothing else would. Conversely, any narrative that frames Sam's father as having been *wrong* to intervene (cowardice-of-courage framings, "your father got himself killed for nothing" provocations) is the most reliably destabilizing rhetorical attack available against him. It has been attempted, in different keys, by the Red Skull, by the Hauser analog, and by various street-level antagonists; Sam's response is consistently a tightened quiet rather than a flare, which is the most diagnostic tell that the wound is live.

---

### 5.2 — Darlene Wilson (mother, deceased)

**History.** Darlene Wilson, killed in a mugging in Harlem approximately two years after Paul's death. The crime was, in the canonical telling, opportunistic and pointless — a small amount of money, a wrong corner, a wrong hour. She was returning from work or from an errand depending on the telling. Sam was, by the most consistent accounts, around ten or eleven; he was not present at the killing but he was the one in the apartment when the news arrived.

**Dynamics in life.** Less rhetorically marked than the father, and that asymmetry is itself important. Where Paul is remembered as voice, Darlene is remembered as *hands and household*: the smell of a kitchen, a particular way of folding a collar, the discipline of getting three children fed and to school in a household that had just lost its primary income and primary public figure. The two years of single motherhood between Paul's death and her own are, in Sam's internal chronology, the period during which he learned the *operational* virtues he later professionalized — making do, scheduling under stress, holding affect steady for the benefit of siblings. The social-work skill set is patrimonially attributed to the father in Sam's conscious narrative but is matrilineally derived in fact.

**Evolution.** The relationship in memory has moved from foregrounded grief (childhood and adolescence) through a long middle period of relative suppression (the Falcon era; Darlene is rarely named in the Englehart/Gerber/DeMatteis runs) into a late re-foregrounding in the Spencer and Coates eras, when Sam — now a public figure being told what kind of Black man he is allowed to be — returns more frequently to the memory of the woman who taught him, in the gap between Paul's death and her own, how to *hold a room* of frightened children without lying to them. The Spencer-era community hotline is, on close reading, a Darlene project as much as a Paul project: the call-and-respond, the patient un-panicking of one caller at a time, is the rhythm of the post-Paul household.

**Trust / safety.** Sam's capacity for *durable, non-performative* intimacy — the long-running relationships with Leila Taylor and Misty Knight, the willingness to stay in a room with someone's grief without trying to fix it — is the Darlene inheritance. The father gave him the public voice; the mother gave him the private attention. The fact that he keeps both functioning in the same career is the integration achievement.

**Failure mode.** Darlene's death is the *unmetabolized* loss. Paul's death has a narrative — minister stepping into violence, dying as he lived. Darlene's death has no narrative; it was random, small, and stupid, and no theology Sam was raised in or has adopted since can dignify it. The failure mode that traces to Darlene is Sam's intolerance of *meaningless* violence specifically — random street crime, opportunistic predation on the vulnerable. His operational choices skew toward containment of exactly this type of harm, and his patience for supervillain-grandiosity rhetoric is shortest when the supervillain in question is hurting ordinary people for trivial gain. This is the file's clearest example of behavior driven by a specific historical wound rather than by general principle.

**Leverage.** Darlene is less weaponizable than Paul because she is less publicly known — the press has a Reverend Wilson file but not a Darlene Wilson file. An adversary who *did* the work and surfaced Darlene specifically (her workplace, her route, the unsolved status of the case if canon supports it) would access a layer of Sam's interior that even Steve Rogers has only glancingly touched. The corresponding tell: Sam will mention his father unprompted in public-facing speech; he will mention his mother only in private, to people he has decided to let see him.

---

### 5.3 — Gideon "Jody" Wilson (older brother, estranged-to-intermittent)

**History.** The older brother appears under variant names across eras (Gideon in some tellings, Jody in others — the file treats them as a single relationship rendered inconsistently by different chroniclers). The constant: an older sibling who did not metabolize the parental deaths the way Sam did, who moved through adolescence with more anger and less structure, who has had at various points a criminal-adjacent or street-economy involvement, and who has surfaced in Sam's life episodically — to ask for help, to express resentment, to disappear again.

**Dynamics.** This is the relationship in which Sam is most consistently *not* the de-escalator. With Gideon, Sam's competence becomes part of the problem: the younger brother who held it together, went to school, built a profession, and now wears a flag, is — from the older brother's vantage — a standing rebuke. Sam knows this and has limited tools to defuse it, because the only defusing move available (diminishing his own life) is one he will not make. Encounters with Gideon are therefore characterized by a Sam who is unusually *tight* — less verbally generative than usual, more given to silences, prone to a particular jaw-set tell that recurs across artists' renderings when the brother is on the page.

**Evolution.** Cyclic. There is a recurring pattern across decades: Gideon resurfaces, asks something (money, help with a legal matter, intervention in a dispute), Sam responds — always at the upper limit of what is prudent, occasionally beyond it — there is a period of cautious re-engagement, then a rupture (usually initiated by Gideon, often around an incident in which Sam's superheroic identity is leveraged or endangered), then years of silence. Sam does not chase him. Sam does not write him off either. The relationship lives in a permanent unresolved tense.

**Trust / safety.** Low operational trust, high familial commitment. Sam will not bring Gideon into operational matters and will not share secure-channel information with him. Sam will also not refuse a call from him.

**Failure mode.** This is where Sam's earned-secure attachment shows its residual anxiety most clearly. He cannot quite enforce the boundary he knows, professionally, he ought to enforce. The over-functioning pattern documented elsewhere in the file is in its purest form here: Sam keeps showing up for a relationship whose terms are set unilaterally by the other party.

**Leverage.** Substantial and known to any competent adversary. Gideon is a vulnerable adult in Sam's orbit, semi-public, lacking Sam's training or institutional protection, and reachable through ordinary street channels. The Red Skull-era playbook and the Spencer-era harassment campaign both touched this perimeter without fully exploiting it. A patient adversary working through Gideon could compromise Sam more efficiently than one working through any of his operational partners. Sam knows this, has thought about it, and has elected — characteristically — not to manage the risk by managing the brother.

---

### 5.4 — Sarah Casper née Wilson (younger sister)

**History.** Sarah is the youngest of the three Wilson siblings, married (Casper), with children, living a deliberately civilian life. She is the family member most consistently rendered across runs as *stable* — employed, partnered, parenting, present.

**Dynamics.** With Sarah, Sam is the older brother he could not be to Gideon — protective, lightly teasing, willing to be the uncle who shows up for the kids' events when operational tempo allows (and audibly apologetic when it does not). The communication baseline is *frequent and low-content*: short calls, check-ins, holiday rituals, the kind of contact that signals presence without requiring crisis. This is the relationship in Sam's life that most closely approximates ordinary middle-aged-sibling normality, and he protects it by *not* bringing his other life into it.

**Evolution.** Increasingly load-bearing as Sam has aged. In the Falcon-era material Sarah is a background figure; in the Spencer and Coates eras she becomes the locus of Sam's post-shield re-grounding — the household he visits when he needs to remember what he is fighting *for* in non-abstract terms. The shift is consistent with the broader maturation arc: a Sam who in his twenties and thirties oriented around his work now, in his operational middle age, orients more visibly around the family that survived.

**Trust / safety.** The highest in the file outside of his operational dyads. Sarah is the person Sam can be tired in front of. She is also, importantly, the person who will tell him he is being stupid — the family member with standing to puncture his self-seriousness, a function Misty Knight performs in the romantic register and Bucky Barnes performs in the operational register but which Sarah uniquely performs in the *fraternal* register.

**Failure mode.** Sam underestimates the cost to Sarah of being his sister in public. She has had to manage a household in which her brother's face is on the news, in which her children's school knows their uncle is Captain America, in which her own civilian privacy is structurally compromised. Sam compensates by minimizing his presence in her public life and maximizing his presence in her private one, but the compensation is partial. The failure mode is *under-acknowledgment*: Sam thanks Sarah less than she deserves because thanking her would make explicit a debt he prefers to keep tacit.

**Leverage.** The single largest leverage point in Sam's life. Sarah and her children are the assets a competent adversary would target first. The Spencer run touched this with the Sons of the Serpent harassment campaigns; the Onyebuchi run elaborated it via the international-trafficking adversaries who learned to look for the family. Sam's countermeasures are layered (location discipline, communication hygiene, occasional Wakandan-sourced security favors) but ultimately insufficient against a peer-level threat, and he lives with that knowledge as a permanent low-grade vigilance.

---

### 5.5 — Sarah's children (nephews; "Jim" and "Jody" in some tellings)

**History.** Two children of school age across most of the canonical record (their ages float by writer; the relationship is what is constant). They know who their uncle is. They have met Steve Rogers. They have, in at least one Spencer-era sequence, been in proximity to operational danger because of their uncle's profile.

**Dynamics.** Sam is, with these children, a different person than he is in any other setting — *unguarded*, physically demonstrative (he is a hugger with the kids in a way he is not with adults), willing to be silly, willing to lose at games on purpose and then occasionally not on purpose. He is also, observably, *teaching* — small lessons about how to handle conflict at school, how to talk to a stranger, how to assess a room — the social worker and the uncle integrated.

**Evolution.** As the children have aged in the comics' elastic chronology, Sam's role has shifted from playful uncle to consultative one. He is the family member they ask the hard questions of, partly because their mother has explicitly designated him as a resource for those questions and partly because he has earned it.

**Trust / safety.** Total in the children's direction; carefully managed in Sam's. He will not lie to them but he will decline to answer, and he has taught them to recognize the difference.

**Failure mode.** Sam's nightmare scenario, never fully articulated on the page but visible in his operational choices, is being responsible for one of these children being hurt because of him. He manages this by maintaining the location and communications discipline noted above, by routing certain categories of threat away from the Casper household at personal cost, and by accepting a permanent low-level guilt about the gap between the uncle he wants to be and the one his operational tempo allows.

**Leverage.** As with Sarah, maximal. The children are the asset class against which Sam's countermeasures are most strained and his composure is most brittle. The one observable behavior that approaches a *rage state* in Sam — and the file is on record that he does not have one — is when these children are credibly threatened. The escalation is still controlled, but the marginal tightening is visible.

---

### 5.6 — Paul Jeffries / "Paul" (estranged or absent figures, lower-confidence)

The canon contains, at various points, references to additional kin — an uncle, a grandparent, a cousin in the South — rendered with insufficient consistency to map at granularity. The pattern across these mentions is that Sam *has* extended family and is *not* close to most of them. Geography (Wilsons largely concentrated in Harlem; Southern relatives at remove) and the disruptive effect of the parental deaths on the family's broader social fabric are sufficient explanation. The behavioral implication is small but real: Sam's family is functionally **nuclear-plus-sister**, not extended-clan, and the emotional load he carries is not distributed across a wide kin network.

---

### 5.7 — The vocational lineage as kin

A file of this scope is obliged to note that Sam's *origin bonds* include a layer that is not consanguineous but is treated, in his interior life, as familial: the line of Black Harlem clergy, social workers, community organizers, and street-level helpers within which his parents lived and into which Sam apprenticed himself professionally. He does not name this lineage as kin in interviews or in private speech, but he behaves toward it as kin — he attends the funerals, he answers the calls, he writes the letters of reference, he shows up at the rallies when shown-up-at-ness is the help being asked for. The Harlem community-hotline work of the Spencer era and the mentorship of Joaquin Torres in the Onyebuchi era are best read as *kinship-coded* extensions of this lineage: Sam reproducing for others the structure of held attention that his parents and their professional cohort provided him in the brief window before it was taken away.

**Trust / safety.** High in the lineage as a whole; calibrated person by person. Sam has been disappointed by individuals in this lineage and has not generalized the disappointment to the lineage.

**Failure mode.** Sam will say yes to community-coded asks past the point of sustainable load, and the depletion that ended his first tenure as Captain America was driven as much by accumulated lineage-obligations as by the public role itself.

**Leverage.** An adversary who could credibly present as a member of this lineage in need — and the Hauser analog, the Americops PR apparatus, and various Hydra-era infiltrations have all attempted variants of this — accesses a category of Sam's obligation that operational discipline does not fully filter. The countermeasure Sam has developed, slowly and at cost, is to verify community asks through the *network* before responding, rather than responding to the ask directly. This is the closest he has come to genuinely revising the over-functioning pattern, and it took him forty in-universe years to develop it.

---

### Summary — the family system

Sam's family of origin was destroyed by street violence inside a four-year window when he was a child. The family he has constructed since — sister, nephews, vocational lineage, and the operational dyads mapped in the next section — is *both* a continuation of and a defense against the family he lost. Every protective behavior in his repertoire is patterned on his mother; every public-facing behavior is patterned on his father; every relational tic with his older brother is patterned on the guilt of having survived intact when an older sibling did not; every interaction with his sister and her children is patterned on the determination that *this* household will not be the one the news arrives at. The remarkable finding remains the integration: Sam has built a life in which these inheritances cohere into a functioning adult instead of fracturing into a damaged one. The cost of the integration is the permanent low-grade vigilance documented throughout this file, and the family chapter is where that vigilance has its source and its meaning.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: SAMUEL THOMAS WILSON
## Section 7 — Relationships II: Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

The methodological note that governs this section: Sam Wilson's relational topology is unusually *legible* by the standards of Marvel principals. He does not hide relationships behind operational compartmentalization (Barnes), behind temporal displacement (Rogers), or behind performed loneliness (Stark). He names his people. He calls them, in the open comm channel, by the names their mothers used. This is itself a clinical finding: a man who treats his connections as load-bearing rather than as vulnerability surfaces. What follows is the granular map.

---

### 7.1 — Steven Grant Rogers

**History.** Met c. *Captain America* #117 (Exiles arc, Skull's Caribbean island scenario), with the originating context — whether one accepts the Snap retcon or not — being that the first thing Sam ever did with Rogers was train with him for combat against an existential enemy. The relationship is therefore *founded on parity in extremis*: Sam was never the awestruck rookie meeting the legend. He met him on an island where they were both expendable. This is the single most important fact about the Sam/Steve dyad and explains why, across fifty-plus in-universe years, Sam is the only person who consistently *talks back* to Rogers without it being a plot of estrangement.

**Dynamics.** The standard misread of the relationship — propagated by press, government, and a not-trivial number of teammates — is that Sam orbits Steve. The textual record is the opposite: Steve orbits Sam emotionally. Rogers is the one who returns to Wilson for grounding; Sam is rarely shown traveling to Rogers for the same. Sam runs the *affective* infrastructure of the friendship: he is the one who initiates check-ins, who makes the joke that breaks the silence after a funeral, who names the thing Steve is avoiding naming. Steve provides tactical-historical depth and the unflinching presence of a man who has decided, in advance, to die for him. The trade is even but asymmetric in kind.

**Trust / safety.** Total in the field, qualified in ideology. Sam will give Steve his back without hesitation; he will not give Steve his vote. The Civil War period is dispositive: Sam stayed with Rogers because of *Steve*, not because Sam agreed with the anti-registration position in the abstract — and he said so. The Coates-era resumption of the Captain America name on his own terms is meaningful precisely because it was not a request for Steve's permission. Sam took the mantle the second time as a peer, not as an heir.

**Evolution.** Three phases. *Bronze Age*: explicit co-billing (the *Captain America and the Falcon* retitle, 1971) that the surrounding apparatus refused to honor; Sam quietly absorbed the indignity while Steve quietly fought against it on his behalf, both with limited success. *Post-*Civil War*–Brubaker*: deepest period of operational intimacy, with Sam functioning as Rogers' field handler during his apparent death and resurrection — Sam mourned him *and* organized the response. *Post-2014 and Spencer/Coates*: structural inversion, with Sam carrying the symbol and Steve initially having to learn how to be the person *behind* it. The Hydra Cap event (Spencer's *Secret Empire*) is the cleanest stress-test: Sam did not become bitter toward Rogers when a counterfeit Rogers betrayed everything; he became *protective* of the real one, which is the marker of a relationship secured at the level of identity rather than behavior.

**Failure mode.** Steve makes a unilateral moral decision that places Sam in an impossible position — and the failure is Sam's, in that he absorbs it. Sam's pattern with Rogers is to express disagreement *once*, clearly, and then to back his play anyway. This is the only relationship in Sam's life where his over-functioning pattern reliably activates, because Rogers triggers Sam's deepest loyalty reflex and his deepest "I will not let the white symbol stand alone" public-image calculus simultaneously.

**Leverage.** Anyone who could credibly threaten Rogers' life or, more potently, Rogers' *legacy*, could move Sam. Threats to Rogers produce in Sam the rare sustained-anger response. Conversely, Rogers' approval is one of the few external validations Sam will accept without examining — which means Rogers is also the person most capable, without intending it, of preventing Sam from leaving a role he should leave. This dynamic has been observed enough times to constitute a structural vulnerability.

---

### 7.2 — James Buchanan Barnes

**History.** Substantive contact begins post–*Winter Soldier* reveal (Brubaker era, mid-2000s) when Barnes is recovering identity and Sam is one of the few people in Rogers' orbit with both the patience and the trauma literacy to be useful in the recovery. Their relationship deepens during the period when Barnes himself carries the shield (2008–2011) and Sam serves as his Falcon-partner with the same professional fidelity he gave Rogers — a fact that should be noted: Sam did not withhold his partnership from the substitute Cap on grounds of loyalty to the original.

**Dynamics.** Comic, abrasive, genuine. The Sam/Bucky dynamic is the most verbally combative of Sam's close relationships and the one most often externally read as friction; it is in fact the most secure. Bucky's humor runs dry and undercutting; Sam's runs warm and provocative; the two meet in a register where neither can be wounded by the other because both are operating in the shared knowledge that they have already seen each other at the absolute worst. Sam is one of perhaps three people in Barnes' life who treats him as a person rather than a case file or a weapon. Barnes is one of perhaps three people in Sam's life who treats him as a *combatant first*, which Sam — exhausted by being treated as the talker — finds restorative.

**Trust / safety.** High and earned. Sam was the first to articulate, in the Brubaker run, the position that Barnes was not responsible for what was done to him, and he said it before Rogers was ready to say it. Barnes remembers this. The trust is bi-directional: Sam will hand Barnes a weapon and turn his back; Barnes will let Sam talk him down from operational decisions Rogers cannot talk him down from.

**Evolution.** From wary professional courtesy (early), through field-tested partnership during the Bucky-Cap era, to the chosen-brother register of the *Falcon & Winter Soldier* material. The arc closes on a Sam who treats Bucky with the easy intimacy of a sibling and a Bucky who, in his own constrained way, has accepted that Sam is family.

**Failure mode.** Barnes relapses — operationally, ideologically, or via external compromise — and Sam over-extends to retrieve him, on the theory that Bucky has earned infinite retrieval. Sam's vulnerability here is the inverse of his vulnerability with Rogers: with Rogers he cannot say no to a yes; with Barnes he cannot give up on a recovery.

**Leverage.** Direct threats to Barnes activate one of Sam's three known sustained-anger states. More subtly: anyone who could weaponize Barnes against Sam (engineer a regression, recreate Winter Soldier conditioning) would land a strike Sam is structurally unprepared to absorb, because his entire relational stance presupposes that Barnes' recovery is real.

---

### 7.3 — Joaquin Torres

**History.** Acquired during the first Sam-as-Cap tenure (Spencer's *Sam Wilson: Captain America*), originally as a migrant teenager caught in a Sons of the Serpent operation, fused with Falcon-DNA via villain experimentation, and effectively orphaned into Sam's orbit. The Onyebuchi *Symbol of Truth* run is the mature articulation: Torres as the new Falcon, Sam as the mentor who *consciously* mentors.

**Dynamics.** This is Sam's only fully *developmental* relationship — every other significant tie is a peer relationship. With Torres, Sam is the elder. The dynamic reveals a Sam who is *patient* in a way he is not with peers, who tolerates failure he would not tolerate in himself, and who deliberately under-instructs in order to let Torres find his own voice. The mentoring style is recognizably the social worker's: Sam does not tell Torres what to think; he asks Torres what he sees.

**Trust / safety.** Sam's trust in Torres is *aspirational* — he trusts him to become the person he is becoming. Torres' trust in Sam is *paternal* in a way Torres himself is too young to fully name. Sam is acutely aware of the asymmetry and works to compress it: he asks Torres' tactical opinion in front of others, defers to Torres' read on Gen-Z cultural terrain, lets Torres make calls Sam himself would make differently.

**Evolution.** From rescue-and-adoption, through sidekick-coded apprenticeship, to genuine partnership with Torres carrying his own arcs. The shift Sam consciously engineered was to *not* repeat with Torres the indignities Sam endured in the Rogers partnership — Torres has been credited, named, given his own missions, and never positioned as decorative.

**Failure mode.** Torres taken, hurt, or killed on a mission Sam authorized. Sam's parental architecture is built on the corpses of his own parents; the loss of a young person under his protection would strike a register no other loss could reach.

**Leverage.** Torres is the single greatest leverage point against Sam in the current operational period. Threats to him produce immediate tactical compromise. This is the most exploitable vulnerability in the file and the one Sam himself is least able to harden, because hardening it would require the kind of emotional distance from Torres that would defeat the purpose of the mentorship.

---

### 7.4 — Mercedes "Misty" Knight

**History.** Long-running intermittent romantic and operational partner from the Bronze Age onward; the relationship's earliest sustained development tracks across the late '70s into the '80s, with revivals in subsequent decades. Misty's own Harlem rootedness, NYPD background, and bionic-arm post-traumatic trajectory make her one of the few people in Sam's life who shares both the geographic and somatic context of his work.

**Dynamics.** Adult, lateral, intermittently romantic, permanently affectionate. Misty is the person with whom Sam does not have to translate Harlem; she is the person with whom Sam does not have to translate the cost of the work; she is the person with whom Sam does not have to translate the experience of being a Black professional navigating predominantly white institutional spaces. The cognitive load that drops in her presence is observable in the textual record — Sam's speech tightens and warms simultaneously, and his humor shifts register toward something more private.

**Trust / safety.** Total. Misty is one of perhaps two people (the other being Sarah, per family file) Sam has ever fully relaxed around. The on-again-off-again pattern of the romance is not a trust failure but a logistics-and-vocation failure: two operators with non-overlapping schedules and overlapping mission profiles.

**Evolution.** From early-career intensity, through the cooling enforced by separate trajectories, to a mature equilibrium of permanent availability without exclusive claim. Both have had other partners; neither has been threatened by it. The current-period dynamic reads as chosen-family-with-romantic-history.

**Failure mode.** Sam's tendency to disappear into the work for extended periods has historically been the relationship's failure point — not infidelity, not betrayal, but absence. Misty has matched this with her own absences, which has stabilized the dyad but at the cost of the kind of daily intimacy neither of them seems to require.

**Leverage.** Misty would be devastating to lose and difficult to threaten — she is hard to leverage *against*, because she is herself a high-functioning operator. The leverage point is subtler: Sam's protective reflex toward her can be triggered into tactical error, and historically has been once or twice in the Priest era.

---

### 7.5 — Riley (deceased, pre-canon in some continuities)

**History.** Original wingman partner from Sam's pre-Falcon military / paramilitary period in the modernized origin variants; the canonical record varies, but the structural fact is consistent: Riley is the partner Sam *lost in the air*, the death that made the wings a memorial as much as an instrument.

**Dynamics.** The dynamic, as preserved in Sam's behavior toward subsequent partners, was *first*-partner intensity — the formative dyad against which all subsequent dyads are unconsciously calibrated. The textual evidence is in Sam's *expectation* in partnership: he assumes partners will be loyal to the death, because the first one was.

**Trust / safety.** Posthumously absolute. Riley is the relationship Sam cannot betray and cannot be betrayed by, because it is closed.

**Evolution.** Static, by definition. The relationship's evolution is entirely in Sam — in what he has done with the loss. The cleanest reading: Riley's death is what made Sam *intentional* about partnership, the trauma that converted an instinct into a discipline.

**Failure mode.** Riley as ghost — the unconscious calibration of subsequent partners to a standard that died young and therefore never disappointed. Torres in particular has had to work, possibly without knowing it, against an impossible comparator.

**Leverage.** Riley's memory cannot be threatened directly but can be invoked manipulatively — anyone who could credibly claim knowledge of Riley's death (especially claiming Sam's responsibility for it) could destabilize him on the specific axis of *was I a good partner*.

---

### 7.6 — T'Challa

**History.** The original Falcon flight rig was Wakandan tech, gifted by T'Challa in the Bronze Age (the canonical hand-off in *Captain America* #170s). The relationship begins, therefore, on a foundation of material generosity from the most powerful Black man in the Marvel cosmos to a Harlem street-level operator. That gift is the structural metaphor for everything that follows.

**Dynamics.** Respectful, lateral despite the obvious resource asymmetry, and characterized by a mutual refusal to instrumentalize each other. T'Challa has never asked Sam to be a proxy for Wakandan interests in the United States; Sam has never asked T'Challa to be the Black-excellence cover for his American operations. Both men understand the other's position is unique and not transferable.

**Trust / safety.** High, with the qualification that the relationship operates at low frequency. They are not in daily contact; they are reliable in crisis. The Onyebuchi *Symbol of Truth* run, with Wakandan-sourced serum trafficking, is the cleanest recent stress-test — Sam navigated a politically combustible scenario without either weaponizing or shielding T'Challa, and T'Challa returned the same professional discretion.

**Trust failure mode.** The Wakandan state apparatus acts in ways that compromise Sam's operational integrity, and T'Challa is unable or unwilling to intervene. This has nearly happened on multiple occasions and is the structural fragility of the relationship.

**Leverage.** Difficult to leverage either direction. The relationship is one of the most stable in either man's file precisely because neither has been willing to test it.

---

### 7.7 — Sarah Wilson (extended-family register; cf. Family file)

**History.** Sister, surviving the family-of-origin trauma alongside Sam. The fuller treatment belongs in the family file; in the chosen-family register, Sarah figures as the relationship that *converts* family-of-origin into chosen family by surviving long enough to become so.

**Dynamics.** The only person to whom Sam reports without editing. Sarah knows the pre-costume Sam, the post-Snap Sam, and the post-shield Sam, and is the only person who has held continuous custody of all three. The dynamic is characterized by Sarah's refusal to be impressed by the cape and Sam's relief at being unimpressive in her presence.

**Trust / safety.** Foundational. The childhood losses they survived together constitute a shared archive no one else can access.

**Failure mode.** Sam's protective architecture extends to Sarah at a level that distorts his judgment; he has historically under-informed her about operational risks in order to protect her psychological terrain, and she has historically resented it.

**Leverage.** Substantial. Sarah and her children, when present in canon, represent the most direct family-of-origin leverage on Sam. See family file for granular detail.

---

### 7.8 — The Harlem Network (collective entry)

**History.** Distinct from any single relationship: Sam's lifelong functional network of Harlem community contacts — pastors at his father's old church, social work colleagues from the New York City system, neighborhood elders, the proprietors of specific bodegas and barbershops on specific blocks, the parents of clients he served decades ago who still call him by his first name. This network is invisible to most of Sam's superhero peers and is the actual ground on which his political and ethical commitments rest.

**Dynamics.** Reciprocal and de-spectacularized. Sam does not enter Harlem as Captain America; he enters as Sam. He buys coffee from the same place, takes the same routes, sits on the same stoops. The network *protects* him in ways that are invisible to surveillance — provides cover, intelligence, and, more importantly, the affective experience of being a person rather than a symbol.

**Trust / safety.** Distributed and resilient. The network has no single point of failure. Sam can lose any individual node without losing the network. This is one of the few operational assets in his life with redundancy.

**Failure mode.** Sam fails the community in a visible, public way — the Spencer-era Americops period was the closest analog — and the network's affective support cools. This was the precipitating context for the shield surrender in 2017: not federal pressure, not press pressure, but the unspoken sense that he was no longer being claimed in his own neighborhood the way he had been.

**Leverage.** Threats to the network produce a categorically different Sam than threats to him personally. He will accept personal risk indefinitely; threats to Harlem move him to operations he would otherwise refuse.

---

### 7.9 — Nick Fury (institutional-allies register)

**History.** Long-running professional contact through Sam's various intersections with S.H.I.E.L.D., never warm, never severed.

**Dynamics.** Adversarial-cooperative. Fury treats Sam with the wary respect Fury reserves for people he cannot fully recruit; Sam treats Fury with the wary respect he reserves for institutional power that has, at moments, been useful. Neither pretends the relationship is friendship.

**Trust / safety.** Operational only. Sam will share intelligence with Fury when interests align and will not when they do not. Fury, by his own account, has been disappointed by Sam's refusal to fully integrate with S.H.I.E.L.D. and has respected it.

**Evolution.** The Spencer-era split from S.H.I.E.L.D. over surveillance disagreements is the formal articulation of a divergence that had been latent for years. Sam did not break with Fury; he broke with the apparatus, and Fury did not take it personally.

**Failure mode.** Fury asks Sam for something Sam cannot refuse without consequence, and Sam refuses anyway. This has happened. It will happen again.

**Leverage.** Fury is one of the few figures who can manufacture *institutional* pressure on Sam — federal investigations, jurisdictional disputes, public records. Sam knows it and has hardened against it.

---

### 7.10 — Carol Danvers, Luke Cage, Storm, and the broader Avengers / hero peer-set (collective entry)

**History.** Decades of intermittent team-ups, alliances, and shared operations across multiple Avengers iterations.

**Dynamics.** Sam is the team member other team members *want* on their flank — universally trusted, universally legible, universally regarded as a stabilizing presence. With Cage specifically: the dynamic is fraternal, with both men sharing the Harlem-rooted Black-superhero context and the experience of being underestimated by the broader hero community. With Danvers: professional warmth, with mutual recognition of each other as the team members who actually run the affective infrastructure others rely on. With Storm: the rarefied mutual recognition of two Black leaders who have each, in their own arenas, carried symbolic weight no one else can fully understand.

**Trust / safety.** Operational trust uniformly high; intimate trust calibrated individually. Sam does not pretend the Avengers are his family; he treats them as his colleagues, with affection.

**Failure mode.** The Avengers as institution demands something of Sam — a public posture, a team vote, a tactical commitment — that contradicts his community obligations. Sam's pattern is to choose the community and accept the institutional consequence. The Gyrich era established the template.

**Leverage.** The peer-set is too broad and too professionally robust to be leveraged collectively. Individually, Cage is the closest to the inner circle in everything but daily contact.

---

### Synthesis — relational architecture

The chosen-family map reveals a Sam Wilson whose intimate infrastructure is *small, deep, and Black-centered at its core*, ringed by a wider professional peer-set characterized by warm operational trust without intimate access. The architecture is unusual among Marvel principals in three respects: (1) it is *intentional* — Sam has chosen these people, has chosen to keep them, has done the affective labor required to keep them, and is conscious of the choice; (2) it is *protected by redundancy* — no single relationship is so load-bearing that its loss would collapse him, except possibly Sarah and possibly, in the current period, Torres; (3) it is *legible to him* — Sam knows what each of these people gives him and what he gives them, and he can articulate it. This is not the relational profile of a man who has stumbled into his connections. It is the profile of a man whose first traumatic loss taught him that connection is the only thing worth building, and who has been building, deliberately, ever since.

The operational implication for surveillance and threat-modeling purposes: Sam Wilson's relationships are not exploitable through deception (he reads people too well) and are only marginally exploitable through corruption (his people are too durable). The exploitable surfaces are the *young* and the *Harlem-rooted*: Torres and the neighborhood. Anyone seeking to move Sam Wilson moves him by threatening what he believes he is responsible for. This has been true since he was nine years old and his father did not come home.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: SAMUEL THOMAS WILSON
## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

A note on the typology before the entries. Sam Wilson's adversary set is unusual in the Marvel principal class: it skews heavily toward **ideological** enemies (people who want to define him, or define America, against his project) rather than **personal** enemies (people who want him dead for their own reasons). His intimate set, conversely, skews toward **peer adults with their own operational lives** — there is no civilian-girlfriend-in-peril pattern in his file, which is itself diagnostic. Sam does not date down, does not date sheltered, and does not maintain relationships in which he is the only one carrying weight.

---

### I. ENEMIES

#### 1. The Red Skull (Johann Shmidt)

**History.** The originating antagonist, in the literal sense: per *Captain America* #186 (Englehart, 1975), the Skull is the architect of the "Snap Wilson" implant — the figure who, via the Cosmic Cube, allegedly authored a personality over Sam's pre-existing one to plant a deep-cover asset close to Rogers. Whether one reads that retcon at face value or as a violation later writers softened, the *subjective* file is unambiguous: the Skull is the man who told Sam Wilson that Sam Wilson was not real.

**Dynamics.** This is the only enemy in Sam's file who attacks him at the level of ontology rather than body or career. Every other adversary tries to defeat, discredit, or kill him; the Skull is the one who tried to *write* him. Sam's behavioral response across decades has been remarkably disciplined: he has never pursued the Skull with the personal vendetta-energy one would predict, and he has never been drawn into the Skull's preferred rhetorical terrain (race, supremacy, the staging of Sam as a propaganda counter-symbol). Sam refuses, structurally, to be the Skull's mirror.

**Evolution.** Englehart-era confrontations were violent and personal; Gruenwald and Brubaker-era Skull appearances generally cast Sam as field opposition without re-relitigating the implant; Spencer's *Secret Empire* (the Hydra-Rogers arc) returned Sam into proximity with Skull-derived ideology in its most insidious form, and Sam's response was characteristically lateral — he did not duel the Skull, he organized the resistance. By the Coates and Onyebuchi eras the Skull functions as a residual specter rather than an active threat, which is appropriate: the wound is permanent and the perpetrator is, for Sam, a closed file he refuses to reopen for sport.

**Trust/Safety.** Zero. The Skull is the one figure in the file Sam will not negotiate with, will not interview, will not de-escalate. This is the rare case where his characteristic talker-first protocol is suspended.

**Failure mode.** Sam's risk with the Skull is not rage; it is the temptation to *re-examine the implant question* in public, which would gratify the Skull's original project. He has consistently declined. The failure mode would be allowing the Skull's framing of his identity to become a topic of his own internal monologue — and the record shows he has walled that off with conscious discipline.

**Leverage.** The Skull's leverage over Sam is the residual ontological doubt — the one question Sam cannot empirically answer about himself. Sam's leverage over the Skull is that, having survived the implant question without breaking, he is living evidence that the Skull's most ambitious psychic weapon failed.

---

#### 2. Stoneface (Lonnie Carver) and the Harlem Street-Level Set

**History.** Stoneface is the recurring Harlem-rooted antagonist of the early Falcon era — a Black gangster with paramilitary aspirations, introduced opposite Sam in *Captain America* #134 (1971) and recurring intermittently. He stands in this file for the entire stratum of Harlem-native criminal adversaries Sam has fought across fifty years: the Tumbler in his earlier appearances, various numbers operators, drug-network principals, and street-gang leadership.

**Dynamics.** This is the most psychologically loaded enemy class in Sam's file, more than the Skull and more than any A-list villain. These are the men Sam grew up among, in some readings *was* (per the Snap retcon), and against whom he must fight without the moral simplicity available to a white hero punching a swastika. Every confrontation with a Stoneface-class figure is also a confrontation with the question of whether Sam's chosen vocation is *betrayal of community* or *service to it* — and writers from Englehart through Priest to Onyebuchi have all let him sit with that question rather than resolve it.

**Evolution.** Early Falcon confrontations were close to standard cape-vs-thug; Priest reframed them with explicit consciousness of the racial-political stakes; Coates and Onyebuchi have largely retired this class of antagonist in favor of state and international actors, which is itself a marker of Sam's evolution out of strictly local operations.

**Trust/Safety.** Operational trust is zero. *Human* recognition is non-zero and consistent. Sam does not dehumanize this class of opponent and does not enjoy putting them down — his after-action behavior in Harlem arrests is markedly subdued compared with his after-action behavior against, e.g., Hydra.

**Failure mode.** The risk here is not over-violence; it is *over-mercy*, or more precisely the tendency to extend rehabilitative attention to street-level operators in ways that have, on file, left openings other adversaries have exploited.

**Leverage.** Their leverage on Sam is community context — the implicit threat of being framed as a race traitor or as a sellout to outside power. Sam's leverage on them is that he genuinely knows the territory, the families, and the economics; he is unbluffable about Harlem in a way no other hero in the canon is.

---

#### 3. Henry Peter Gyrich

**History.** Not technically an "enemy" in the cape-versus-villain sense, but functionally one of the most consequential adversaries in Sam's life. The federal liaison who, in *Avengers* #181–184 (1979), imposed Sam on the Avengers roster as an affirmative-action mandate over Sam's objection and over the team's objection — instrumentalizing him as racial cover and as a lever against Iron Man's leadership.

**Dynamics.** Gyrich's offense is the prototype of the structural offense Sam experiences repeatedly: being used as a symbol against his own values, with his Blackness as the instrument. Sam's response was the defining behavioral template — he served, performed competently, refused to perform gratitude, and quit on his own terms. He did not punch Gyrich. He did not denounce Gyrich publicly. He simply withdrew and refused to be re-recruited under those conditions.

**Evolution.** Gyrich's appearances opposite Sam over the decades have been intermittent and have followed the same pattern: bureaucratic instrumentalization, Sam's flat refusal, Sam's exit. The dynamic has not evolved because Gyrich has not evolved; Sam treats him as a fixed feature of the institutional landscape, like weather.

**Trust/Safety.** Zero institutional trust. Sam will not accept a Gyrich-brokered assignment without independent verification of every premise.

**Failure mode.** Historically, *accepting the seat* — the original Gyrich Avengers tenure was itself the failure mode, and Sam has since calibrated. The risk persists in milder form: Sam's over-functioning under institutional disrespect is a Gyrich-shaped vulnerability that will recur whenever the institution finds the right pressure.

**Leverage.** Gyrich's leverage is access to platforms Sam genuinely wants in service of his project. Sam's leverage is that Gyrich, and the apparatus he represents, cannot find a Black symbol of equivalent legitimacy who is willing to be instrumentalized — Sam's refusals constrain Gyrich's optionality.

---

#### 4. The Hauser Analog and the Right-Wing Media Apparatus

**History.** Spencer's *Captain America: Sam Wilson* (2015–2017) introduced the in-fiction conservative radio host as the running antagonist of Sam's tenure with the shield — a figure who treated Sam's Captain America publicly as illegitimate, racially miscast, and politically suspect, and who organized audience hostility into operational consequence.

**Dynamics.** Unlike a costumed villain, this enemy cannot be punched. The medium of attack is reputational and the medium of damage is psychic and political. Sam's response was, characteristically, to refuse the bait while doing the work — but the cumulative load of being adjudicated daily by a hostile mass audience is the documented proximate cause of the shield surrender in 2017.

**Evolution.** Post-2017, Sam has held subsequent media cycles at greater operational distance — Coates' Sam reads the press without engaging it, Onyebuchi's Sam treats international press as a separate problem from American press and triages accordingly. The lesson absorbed is: this class of enemy cannot be defeated, only outlasted, and the outlasting has a body count in the self.

**Trust/Safety.** Zero. Sam will not grant on-record interviews to this stratum.

**Failure mode.** The crack in Sam's regulation under chronic media attack is the most documented behavioral vulnerability in his file. He does not snap; he *attritions*. Given enough time and enough volume, the channel through which damage enters is exhaustion, not anger.

**Leverage.** Their leverage is volume and reach. Sam's leverage — which the Spencer run shows him learning to use — is that he can simply stop engaging, and the apparatus needs his engagement to generate content.

---

#### 5. The Sons of the Serpent and the Americops

**History.** Two intertwined Spencer-era adversary structures: the Sons as the recurring white-supremacist paramilitary, the Americops as the privatized police force whose abuse patterns Sam confronted directly. Both are programmatic rather than personal — neither has a single antagonist face.

**Dynamics.** These are the enemies who taught Sam that the shield-as-symbol could be deployed against the very communities he had taken it up to protect, and that the people doing the deploying would invoke patriotism, law, and order in the doing. The Americops confrontations in particular show Sam at his most operationally restrained and politically explicit — refusing to fight police officers as such, while obstructing specific abuses and exposing the system.

**Evolution.** The category has expanded outward in Onyebuchi's run to international analogs — state and quasi-state actors using ostensibly legitimate force to traffic, exploit, or suppress. Sam's posture has matured: he no longer expects to dismantle these systems and he no longer mistakes individual interdictions for systemic victories.

**Trust/Safety.** Zero. These are the enemies most likely to be wearing a uniform Sam is institutionally supposed to respect.

**Failure mode.** Sam's risk here is the inverse of his street-level risk — *over-restraint* in the face of state-sanctioned violence, the temptation to operate within rules of engagement the adversary is not honoring.

**Leverage.** Their leverage is legal cover and the framing of Sam's interference as criminal. Sam's leverage is moral clarity that survives public adjudication.

---

#### 6. Hydra-Coded Rogers (the *Secret Empire* Configuration)

**History.** Spencer's *Secret Empire* (2017) configured Steve Rogers as a Cosmic Cube-altered Hydra operative who seized the United States. Sam was the principal organizer of the resistance.

**Dynamics.** This is the singular catastrophic event in Sam's adult life — the person closest to him, the partner whose legitimacy he had inherited the shield from, weaponized against the project they had shared. The cruelty of it is precise: Sam, the man who knew firsthand what a Cosmic Cube could do to a personality, was forced to fight a Cube-altered Rogers, and to do it while a hostile public assumed *Sam* was the false article and Rogers the true one.

**Evolution.** Post-restoration, Sam's relationship with the actual Rogers (see Relationships II) was reconstructed with admirable bandwidth, but the file shows a measurable residue: a flicker of conditional vigilance, an awareness that the man he loves is also a person who can, under sufficiently exotic pressure, be made into a weapon against him.

**Trust/Safety.** Toward the Hydra-coded configuration: zero, and operationally lethal. Toward the restored Rogers: restored, with notation.

**Failure mode.** Sam's risk in any future identity-overwrite scenario is *recognizing the pattern too late* — the prior experience of the Snap question has made him paradoxically reluctant to accuse others of being not-themselves.

**Leverage.** The dynamic is not really one of leverage but of grief; this is the enemy Sam mourned even while fighting.

---

#### 7. The Serum-Trafficking Network (Onyebuchi Era)

**History.** *Symbol of Truth* (2022–2023) opposed Sam against an international network trafficking Wakandan-derived super-soldier serum, with state actors and private operators on every side.

**Dynamics.** This adversary class is the mature-state opposition Sam has grown into: transnational, multi-jurisdictional, morally legible but operationally complex. Sam fights it as a *Captain America who knows the symbol does not travel cleanly across borders* — and the file shows him doing real diplomatic and improvisational work to operate in jurisdictions where the shield is suspect or hostile.

**Evolution.** This is the current state of Sam's enemy frontier and represents his furthest evolution from the original Harlem street-level brief: from neighborhood to nation to network.

**Trust/Safety.** Operational only, on a per-actor basis. Sam treats this domain as a series of negotiations rather than a war.

**Failure mode.** Over-extension. Sam at international scale is operating beyond the relational density that has historically protected him; he has fewer people who actually know him in this theater, and his characteristic room-reading is degraded by language, culture, and lack of context.

**Leverage.** Sam's leverage in this theater is unexpected — the symbol he carries is read very differently abroad than at home, and in many contexts he finds himself trusted by parties who would never trust an American official. He has begun, consciously, to use this.

---

### II. RIVALS

#### 8. John Walker (the alternate-Captain figure)

**History.** Originally introduced as the Super-Patriot in *Captain America* #323 (1986, Gruenwald) and subsequently elevated to and broken by the shield, Walker is the file's most important *rival* — not enemy, not friend, but the man whose existence triangulates what kind of Captain America Sam is.

**Dynamics.** Walker is everything Sam is not: enhanced, white, aggressive, externally regulated, prone to escalation, and ideologically committed to a version of America Sam serves only conditionally. The two have operated in the same adjacency more than once, and the file shows Sam exercising a kind of careful, ungenerous patience with Walker — neither writing him off nor pretending to ally with him, treating him as a man whose damage is real and whose dangerousness is also real.

**Evolution.** Across iterations, Sam's posture toward Walker has shifted from wary professionalism to something approaching grudging coalition under specific conditions. The defining contrast remains: Walker breaks outward, Sam withdraws inward; Walker needs the shield to organize him, Sam can put it down and remain himself.

**Trust/Safety.** Conditional and revocable. Sam will work alongside Walker on bounded missions and will not turn his back on him in ambiguous ones.

**Failure mode.** The risk is being drawn into adjudicating Walker — Sam's instinct to mentor and de-escalate runs against the diagnostic reality that Walker is not, in the relevant senses, salvageable on Sam's timetable.

**Leverage.** Walker's leverage on Sam is the implicit comparison — every time Walker fails violently, Sam is asked to be the corrective image, which is its own burden. Sam's leverage on Walker is that he simply is what Walker is being measured against.

---

#### 9. Tony Stark

**History.** Rival rather than enemy, and only intermittently rival. The relationship dates to the Gyrich-era Avengers period (Tony was the team leader when Sam was imposed) and runs through *Civil War* and after.

**Dynamics.** Sam and Tony disagree about almost every structural question — surveillance, centralization, registration, the role of technology in supplanting community judgment. Sam's *Civil War* posture (anti-registration, with Rogers) was ideologically prior to his loyalty to Rogers; he would have opposed the SHRA on its merits. Yet the file does not show personal animus. Sam treats Tony as a man whose intelligence he respects and whose conclusions he rejects, and Tony treats Sam as one of the few opponents whose objections he cannot dismiss as parochial.

**Evolution.** The rivalry has cooled into something like mutual professional respect with continuing structural disagreement. The Spencer-era surveillance split from S.H.I.E.L.D. is, at one remove, a continuation of the Tony-axis argument.

**Trust/Safety.** Operational trust high; institutional trust low. Sam will fight alongside Tony and will not sign Tony's papers.

**Failure mode.** Sam's risk with Tony is *underestimating the speed of escalation* — Tony's institutional moves arrive faster than Sam's communitarian response cycle, and Sam has historically been on the back foot in the politics of these confrontations.

**Leverage.** Tony's leverage is resources and reach. Sam's leverage is moral legibility — when the public can hear both, Sam wins the room.

---

#### 10. Nick Fury (and the S.H.I.E.L.D. Apparatus)

**History.** Not an enemy, formally a sometime ally, but the file demands inclusion here because the *institutional* relationship is structurally adversarial. Sam's split from S.H.I.E.L.D. in the early Spencer run was the operational expression of a long-running disagreement about surveillance, jurisdiction, and the proper relationship between federal force and local community.

**Dynamics.** Sam works with Fury and Hill when missions require it; he does not subordinate to them, and he has been explicit on-page about why. The disagreement is principled, not personal.

**Trust/Safety.** Personal trust calibrated per individual; institutional trust low and conditional.

**Failure mode.** The risk is being read by the apparatus as a defector rather than an independent — Sam's refusals have, on file, generated operational friction he did not seek.

**Leverage.** Their leverage is information and asset access. Sam's leverage is legitimacy in domains S.H.I.E.L.D. cannot operate in without him.

---

### III. INTIMATES AND LOVERS

#### 11. Leila Taylor

**History.** The defining relationship of the early-to-mid Falcon era. Leila — a Harlem activist with a Black-nationalist orientation more politically radical than Sam's — was Sam's significant partner across much of the 1970s, introduced in *Captain America* #143 (1971) and recurring substantially through the Englehart and Gruenwald eras.

**Dynamics.** Leila was the relationship that made Sam *justify his work to the community he was working for*. Her politics ran to the left of his, her patience for the Avengers and the federal apparatus was short, and her skepticism of his partnership with Rogers was structural and articulate. The file shows Sam *listening* to her in a way he did not listen to white peers offering the same critiques — Leila's reach into his interior was specific and earned.

**Evolution.** The relationship was on-and-off across years, with the rupture lines tracking ideological more than emotional disagreements. It did not end in betrayal or violence; it ended in different operational lives.

**Trust/Safety.** Personal trust full. Operational trust high but bounded by political disagreement.

**Failure mode.** Sam's pattern with Leila was *attempted reconciliation through harder work* — taking on more of the kinds of cases she would approve of, in the hopes the relational tension would resolve. It did not. This is an early signature of the over-functioning-as-relationship-repair pattern.

**Leverage.** Leila held the community-legitimacy lever, which is, per the personality file, the deepest of Sam's pressure points. She used it carefully and rarely.

---

#### 12. Misty Knight

**History.** The longest-running and most operationally compatible intimate relationship in Sam's file. Misty — ex-NYPD, bionic-armed, founding principal of Heroes for Hire, fiercely independent — has been a recurring partner across decades, including substantial portions of the Priest, Brubaker, and post-Coates eras.

**Dynamics.** This is the relationship that fits Sam best because Misty is structurally his peer: same race, same urban operational context, comparable trauma load, comparable refusal-to-be-handled energy, and a comparable insistence on her own work. There is no caretaker imbalance, no one-up/one-down, and crucially no community-legitimacy gap — Misty does not have to be sold on Sam's project. They share it.

**Evolution.** Intermittent rather than continuous, but the pattern is reunion-friendly: Sam and Misty have re-converged multiple times without the relational debris that usually accumulates across long on-off intimacies. The file reads this as evidence of unusually clean prior endings.

**Trust/Safety.** Personal trust full. Operational trust full — one of the very small number of people Sam will let into a high-stakes mission with no read-in and trust to read it correctly on arrival.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's structural risk is that two people of this operational profile are rarely in the same theater long enough to sustain continuous intimacy. The failure mode is not rupture but *attenuation*.

**Leverage.** Mutual and symmetrical. Misty is, on present evidence, the person in the file with the highest combined personal-and-operational standing in Sam's life — Rogers excepted, and the comparison is meaningfully different because Rogers' standing is comradely while Misty's is intimate.

---

#### 13. Riley (Pre-Canon and Backstory)

**History.** A name carried across some iterations as the lost military wingman whose death predates Sam's superheroic career — variably canonized depending on continuity, but present in enough of the file (and structurally implied even where unnamed) to warrant entry as a *grief object* rather than as an active relationship.

**Dynamics.** Where written, Riley occupies the slot of Sam's lost peer-brother — the relationship whose ending shaped Sam's protective stance toward subsequent partners (notably Torres). The file shows Sam carrying this loss without exhibiting it: no anniversaries publicly observed, no shrines, no operational impairment in similar mission profiles. The grief is metabolized but real.

**Trust/Safety.** Not applicable (deceased); the residual is Sam's elevated protectiveness toward any partner occupying a structurally similar role.
